<td>ᙿ
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are additional finals: allophones <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ᑊ&#x27e9;</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[h]</span> and <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ᐦ&#x27e9;</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[x]</span>, and three medials: <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ᖿᐧ&#x27e9;</span> <span title="Blackfoot-language romanization"><i lang="bla-Latn">ksa</i></span>, <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ᒣᐧ&#x27e9;</span> <span title="Blackfoot-language romanization"><i lang="bla-Latn">tsa</i></span>, <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ᖿᑉ&#x27e9;</span> <span title="Blackfoot-language romanization"><i lang="bla-Latn">kya</i></span>, <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ᖿᙿ&#x27e9;</span> <span title="Blackfoot-language romanization"><i lang="bla-Latn">kwa</i></span>.
</p><p><span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;᙮&#x27e9;</span> is used for a period.
</p><p>Also, sometimes it is written in Latin letters but with different spelling on computers because not all computers support the letters used in the Blackfoot language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Literature"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Tims" title="John Tims">John Tims</a> was an Anglican clergyman with the <a href="/wiki/Church_Missionary_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Church Missionary Society">Church Missionary Society</a>. He was at <a href="/wiki/Blackfoot_reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackfoot reserve">Blackfoot reserve</a> from 1883 to 1895. Tims translated parts of the Bible into Blackfoot. Selections from Matthew were published by the Church Missionary Society Mission Press in 1887. The Gospel of Matthew was published by the <a href="/wiki/British_and_Foreign_Bible_Society" title="British and Foreign Bible Society">British and Foreign Bible Society</a> in 1890, and other portions of Scripture were published as Readings from the Holy Scriptures by the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Promoting_Christian_Knowledge" title="Society for Promoting Christian Knowledge">Society for Promoting Christian Knowledge</a> in 1890. He used both Roman script and a <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Aboriginal_syllabics" title="Canadian Aboriginal syllabics">Canadian Aboriginal syllabics</a> script. The Gospel of Mark was translated by Donald G. Frantz and Patricia Frantz, and published by Scriptures Unlimited, a joint venture of the New York Bible Society (later called Biblica) and the World Home Bible League (later called the Bible League) in 1972. The Gospel of John was Translated by Wycliffe Bible Translators and Blackfoot people and published by the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_Bible_Society&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Canadian Bible Society (page does not exist)">Canadian Bible Society</a> in 1979.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_endangerment">Causes of endangerment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Causes of endangerment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Negative attitude towards Blackfoot is the primary reason for endangerment.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Most children were discouraged from speaking the minority language in schools or public places.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Children were often beaten for speaking their native language and were sent home.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Teachers were very ashamed when their students spoke their native language.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitality">Vitality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Vitality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the UNESCO Factor 1: Intergenerational Transmission, Blackfoot is classified under severely endangered.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> It is predicted that Blackfoot is used mostly by the grandparental generation and up.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In fact, there are no more than 1500 native speakers, most of whom are likely over the age of 50.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Due to the lack of speakers, the language will likely be extinct within the next 40 years. Once older people pass away, no one will be speaking Blackfoot unless something changes.
</p><p>According to the UNESCO Factor 9: Amount and Quality of Documentation, there may be adequate grammar or sufficient amount of grammar, dictionaries, and texts.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> For example, a secondary documentation exists today, <i>Blackfoot Dictionary of Stems, Roots and Affixes, 3rd Edition</i> (2017), written by Donald G. Frantz and Norma Jean Russell.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The newest edition includes more than 1,000 new entries, major additions to verb stems, contains more than 5,500 Blackfoot–English entries, and an English index of more than 6,000 entries.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The transcription uses an official, technically accurate alphabet and the authors of this book have classified entries and selected examples based on more than 46 years of research.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> This book is comprehensive and includes enough information for those who wish to learn Blackfoot and for those who have an interest in Native Studies and North American linguistics.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revitalization_efforts">Revitalization efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Revitalization efforts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the late 1900s, many tribes began a surge of revitalization efforts to encourage cultural awareness of indigenous customs and traditions. Of these, the Blackfoot revitalization effort has proven to be quite successful, producing various institutions, including a college dedicated to preserving and promoting Blackfoot traditions. Today, there are head-start programs in primary and secondary schools on the reservation to teach even infants and toddlers about the history of the tribe from an early age.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Piegan_Institute">The Piegan Institute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: The Piegan Institute"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1987, Dorothy Still Smoking and Darrell Robes Kipp founded the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Piegan_Institute&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Piegan Institute (page does not exist)">Piegan Institute</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> a private 501(c)(3) non-profit foundation in Montana dedicated to researching, promoting, and preserving the Native American Languages, particularly the Blackfoot language. Piegan Institute founded Nizipuhwahsin (also Nizi Puh Wah Sin or Niitsípuwahsin or Cuts Wood) School in 1995 as a Blackfoot language K–8 immersion school. Since its inception the school has grown and relocated to the center of Browning, Montana, in a custom-built schoolhouse. Recently, some of the school's first graduates have returned to teach the newest generation the Blackfoot language.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blackfeet_Community_College">Blackfeet Community College</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Blackfeet Community College"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Blackfeet_Community_College" title="Blackfeet Community College">Blackfeet Community College</a> (BCC), founded in 1974, is a two-year, nationally accredited college that was made possible by the Indian Education Act of 1972 and the 1964 Act enacted by the Office of Economic Opportunity. BCC is a member of both the American Indian Higher Education Consortium and the American Indian Science and Engineering Society (AISES). It allows teenagers and adults alike to take classes in a wide range of subjects, from classes in Psychology and Digital Photography to classes on Blackfoot language and tradition. They have beginning Blackfoot language classes with labs for members and non-members of the community to learn the language.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chief_Mountain_Technologies">Chief Mountain Technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Chief Mountain Technologies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In order to create jobs for the Blackfoot people with real-world applications, the Blackfeet Tribal Business Council launched a company called Chief Mountain Technologies in 2009. This company gives tribal members the opportunity to work in the fields of computer science and business in Browning, Montana, on behalf of various government organizations. The establishment of this company in the Blackfoot community allows the people to use their culture and their language in the modern world while maintaining their traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_programming_in_Blackfoot">Radio programming in Blackfoot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Radio programming in Blackfoot"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Radio station <a href="/wiki/KBWG-LP" title="KBWG-LP">KBWG</a> in <a href="/wiki/Browning,_Montana" title="Browning, Montana">Browning, Montana</a>, broadcasts a one-hour show for Blackfoot language learners four times a week.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The Voice of Browning, Thunder Radio, FM 107.5, or <span title="Blackfoot-language romanization"><i lang="bla-Latn">Ksistsikam ayikinaan</i></span> (literally 'voice from nowhere') went live in 2010, and focuses on positive programming. In 2011, John Davis, a 21-year-old Blackfeet Community College student explained, "I was the first Blackfeet to ever talk on this radio", Davis said. "This is my <a href="/wiki/Counting_coup" title="Counting coup">coup story</a>." A story in the <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Falls_Tribune" title="Great Falls Tribune">Great Falls Tribune</a></i> noted, "When the station was replaying programming that originated elsewhere, the radio was all '<a href="/wiki/There%27s_a_Tear_in_My_Beer" title="There&#39;s a Tear in My Beer">tear in my beer</a>' and '<a href="/wiki/Your_Cheatin%27_Heart_(Hank_Williams,_Jr._album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Your Cheatin&#39; Heart (Hank Williams, Jr. album)">your cheatin' heart</a>.' They called it the suicide station for its depressing old country themes<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..." The station's offerings have now expanded beyond country to include AC/DC and Marvin Gaye, and "on-the-air jokes they would never hear on a <a href="/wiki/Clear_Channel_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Clear Channel Communications">Clear Channel</a> radio station, such as: 'The captain is as cool as <a href="/wiki/Government_cheese" title="Government cheese">commodity cheese</a>.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"So far we have broadcasting Monday through Friday from around 6:30, Indian time", quipped station manager Lona Burns, "to around 11, Indian time." ... "It's Indian radio", agreed Running Crane. "Where else can you hear today's hits with <a href="/wiki/Native_American_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Native American music">traditional music</a>?"<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canadian_government_support">Canadian government support<span class="anchor" id="Writing_System"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Canadian government support"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Canadian government has provided support for the languages through funds and other financial resources. According to James Moore, the former Minister of Canadian Heritage and Official Languages, "the Government of Canada is committed to the revitalization and preservation of Aboriginal languages." The funding was put to use in the form of digital libraries containing interviews with native speakers, online courses, and various other resources in the hopes of promoting Blackfoot language and passing it down to subsequent generations. On top of both of these government efforts, the Canadian Government has also provided over $40,000 through the Aboriginal Languages Initiative Fund to promote the use of Aboriginal languages in community and family settings.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019 the Canadian government announced their investment of over $1.5 million towards supporting Indigenous languages in Southern Alberta. Eleven out of seventeen projects approved for funding focus on revitalization of the Blackfoot language and include efforts such as language classes, illustrated workbooks, graphic novels, a video game, and a mobile app.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to federal funding, the Blackfoot language is also supported through Alberta's Indigenous Languages in Education grant program. This program offers up to $285,000 annually towards the development of Indigenous language training, programs, and curricula for instructors between Kindergarten and Grade 12, and an additional $50,000 annually towards development of new resources for Indigenous language teaching and learning.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Piikani_Traditional_Knowledge_Services">Piikani Traditional Knowledge Services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Piikani Traditional Knowledge Services"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Piikani Traditional Knowledge Services serve the Piikani Nation as the first stop in understanding the development of meaningful relationships with the Indigenous Nation.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Their vision is to enhance, preserve, protect, and be keepers of the Piikani culture, language, spirituality, songs, customs, and history.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This program is committed to sustaining and preserving <span title="Blackfoot-language romanization"><i lang="bla-Latn">Piikanisinni</i></span>, the way of life of the Piikani, that identifies characteristic values, principles, and integrity maintained from ancient Piikani culture and practices.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The program includes Piikanissi cultural education and training, Piikanissini cultural mobilization, Piikanissini data management, and provides resources that promote <span title="Blackfoot-language romanization"><i lang="bla-Latn">Piikanissini</i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This program is community-based and focuses on keeping the Blackfoot culture and language alive.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Blackfoot_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: References"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
